<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer17518920480144813613"><div>Big men. Big money. Big games. Big libidos. Big trouble.<br /><br />A decade ago, <em>The Bonfire of the Vanities</em> defined an era--and established Tom Wolfe as our prime fictional chronicler of America at its most outrageous and alive. This time the setting is Atlanta, Georgia--a racially mixed late-century boomtown full of fresh wealth, avid speculators, and worldly-wise politicians. The</div></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17518920480144813613"><div>Big men. Big money. Big games. Big libidos. Big trouble.<br /><br />A decade ago, <em>The Bonfire of the Vanities</em> defined an era--and established Tom Wolfe as our prime fictional chronicler of America at its most outrageous and alive. This time the setting is Atlanta, Georgia--a racially mixed late-century boomtown full of fresh wealth, avid speculators, and worldly-wise politicians. The protagonist is Charles Croker, once a college football star, now a late-middle-aged Atlanta real-estate entrepreneur turned conglomerate king, whose expansionist ambitions and outsize ego have at last hit up against reality. Charlie has a 28,000-acre quail-shooting plantation, a young and demanding second wife--and a half-empty office tower with a staggering load of debt. When star running back Fareek Fanon--the pride of one of Atlanta's grimmest slums--is accused of raping an Atlanta blueblood's daughter, the city's delicate racial balance is shattered overnight. Networks of illegal Asian immigrants crisscrossing the continent, daily life behind bars, shady real-estate syndicates, cast-off first wives of the corporate elite, the racially charged politics of college sports--Wolfe shows us the disparate worlds of contemporary America with all the verve, wit, and insight that have made him our most phenomenal, most admired contemporary novelist.<br />
</div></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17518920480144813613">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_3387428">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3387428" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/147280-wilson" class="left imgcol" title="Wilson"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1255885338p2/147280.jpg" alt="Wilson" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/3387428?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 22, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/147280-wilson" class="user" title="Wilson">Wilson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer3387428">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15550506079343564761">Some crappy books are forgettable. A Man in Full still haunts me like a chain-jangling ghost of crappiness. The plot is hamfisted, the "twists" are ludicrous (for a book with the pretensions of this one), and what is supposed to be a look-behind-the-curtain of Atlanta winds up instead being at times an infomercial on Stoicism, a horse-humping voyeur's dream, and a look at the intricacies of west coast meat packing. A part that really bugs me is that there is a line in which one of the characters</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15550506079343564761">Some crappy books are forgettable. A Man in Full still haunts me like a chain-jangling ghost of crappiness. The plot is hamfisted, the "twists" are ludicrous (for a book with the pretensions of this one), and what is supposed to be a look-behind-the-curtain of Atlanta winds up instead being at times an infomercial on Stoicism, a horse-humping voyeur's dream, and a look at the intricacies of west coast meat packing. A part that really bugs me is that there is a line in which one of the characters groans over a particular great sexual encounter in which the lady in question did "that thing with the cup!" The same line is also in the Bonfire of the Vanities (which I read directly preceding this). What thing with the cup? Whatever it is, I'm pretty sure it's not that pleasant. And to have male characters gasping about it's majesty in two books back to back means that either 1) Tom is giving a sly wink to his fans with a nonsense line in which case, no thanks, Tom, that's a wink of recognition I don't need or 2) Tom is so dottering and so freakishly in to that "thing with the cup" that he forgot he put it in Bonfire but was like, "You know what would really round out this female character's sexiness? A reference to 'that thing with the cup' that I love so much and I ask all the girls I party with for." Revolting.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15550506079343564761">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_140177842">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/140177842" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3268309-steve-lowe" class="left imgcol" title="Steve Lowe"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1427727266p2/3268309.jpg" alt="Steve Lowe" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/140177842?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 03, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3268309-steve-lowe" class="user" title="Steve Lowe">Steve Lowe</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer140177842">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2380871287782928537">Dear Tom Wolfe,<br /><br />I am writing to you now about your insistence on describing what every character is wearing in every scene of your 741-page epic, <em>A Man in Full</em>. I am convinced that, had you chosen to avoid doing such a thing as describe each cut of cloth, each brand name, each sartorial style, and its significance based on the character or the setting, etc., this book could have been a much crisper, much leaner, much more <em>manly</em> 400 pages.<br /><br />Sincerely, Steve<br /><br />But I guess that’s what you get when a fopp</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2380871287782928537">Dear Tom Wolfe,<br /><br />I am writing to you now about your insistence on describing what every character is wearing in every scene of your 741-page epic, <em>A Man in Full</em>. I am convinced that, had you chosen to avoid doing such a thing as describe each cut of cloth, each brand name, each sartorial style, and its significance based on the character or the setting, etc., this book could have been a much crisper, much leaner, much more <em>manly</em> 400 pages.<br /><br />Sincerely, Steve<br /><br />But I guess that’s what you get when a foppish little dandy such as this:<br /><br /><span>[image error]</span><br /><br />… writes a book about what it is to be a man, or at least a portrait, or cross section if you will, of the state of manhood in the late 1990s south (Atlanta to be specific).<br /><br />Let me say it this way: I don’t give a flying fuck what kind of suit your character is wearing from day to day. Maybe if it has some significance whatsoever to the plot, then that’s OK. But when you pull the reader aside so you can point out that a lawyer about to face a clamoring mob of reporters was afraid, and “not even his flawless sartorial armor – a new hard-finished worsted navy suit from Gus Carroll, a high-necked tab-collar shirt, a pale-blue crepe de chine necktie with a <em>perfect</em> dimple – could make him feel secure…” well, then I say, fuck you. I DON’T CARE! I’m guessing that’s what Tom Wolfe was wearing when he wrote that scene. In fact, by the looks of the guy, I’m guessing he changed his clothes every time he changed characters.<br /><br />The characters, by the way, are the strength of this book, if you’re wondering. If you look past the convenient plot turns and less than surprising twists, the reliance on coincidence, and the fairly stereotypical and rudimentary categories of men (nearly every white man over 40 was either half bald or had “buttery jowls”, and quite often both), once Tommy Boy really gets into the character, he really gets in. Each character has a distinct voice, which is impressive since we follow along fairly closely with Charlie Croker (old, white, racisty real estate developer who’s about to lose everything), Roger (Too) White II (light-skinned African American lawyer who is almost “too white”), Conrad Hensley (the heart and soul of the book, a young man who loses everything after he’s laid off from one of Croker’s food warehouses), Martha Croker (Charlie’s first wife, who battles with her relevance in Atlanta’s social scene after being cast away by Charlie), and Raymond Peepgass (middle-aged loser who works at the bank going after Charlie and trying to be more than a meek little fish in a big pond).<br /><br />Now, if Tommy Boy could have just gotten over his hang up with clothes, I would have liked this one so much better. If you like to dress fancy and spend all of your royalties on clothes, that’s fine. But don’t go mucking up what was otherwise a fine, well-written, engrossing novel with all of that bullshit. That cost you a star, Tom Wolfe. It cost you a star.<br /><br />By the way, as I rapped out this review, I sat comfortably in my Faded Glory gray thermal long-sleeve tee (on clearance at Meijer, I think) and a pair of J. Crew pants that I got for free from the college kids who used to live across the street from us and were throwing out a bunch of clothes the day they moved out, and on to jobs in places like Knoxville and Cleveland, where they would no doubt be expected to wear clothing that was in some way worsted and lawyerly. In case you were wondering…<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2380871287782928537">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/21134-daniel" class="user" title="Daniel">Daniel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer910647">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5522607888759715595">Ah, what to say about this book that the other reviewers haven't already said?<br /><br />One thing - this book seems to be present wherever used books are sold. Every old shop has a surplus of them. It's always on the dollar shelves at book sales. Even at the local thrift stores, it's there, sitting right next to Lonesome Dove and Tim Allen's autobiography.<br /><br />This book must find its way to second-hand shelves because it's both big AND mainstream. In the age of quick-selling novellas, there aren't many authors</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5522607888759715595">Ah, what to say about this book that the other reviewers haven't already said?<br /><br />One thing - this book seems to be present wherever used books are sold. Every old shop has a surplus of them. It's always on the dollar shelves at book sales. Even at the local thrift stores, it's there, sitting right next to Lonesome Dove and Tim Allen's autobiography.<br /><br />This book must find its way to second-hand shelves because it's both big AND mainstream. In the age of quick-selling novellas, there aren't many authors who dare to produce leviathan-sized novels. Sure, there's Thomas Pynchon, Don DeLillo, and David Foster Wallace. They all have their own digressive coffee-house style, their fanciful artsyness. Some people read their novels for bragging rights. They place them in conspicuous spots as conversation pieces. When you finish "Against the Day," or "Underworld," or "Mason-Dixon," you don't want to forget your accomplishment. Some prefer to revel in it and lord it over others. Perhaps this is why Wallace and Pynchon don't make it to thrift store shelves as frequently as Wolfe.<br /><br />"A Man in Full" is a different kind of big book. It is extremely earnest and unaffected. In some ways, it belongs more to the traditions of the 19th century than to the 20th. <br /><br />Tom Wolfe avoids the narrative flourishes of his contemporaries and uses old-fashioned and unsubtle plot devices. The cast of characters is Dickensian - there's a tycoon, a young worker, a scheming banker, a well-heeled lady, and a debutante. There's a prison break, a bankers' shakedown, many backroom deals, dramatic changes of heart. Wolfe uses lots of satirical wordplay, which when applied to 20th century popular culture seems slightly contrived (viz. Wolfe's take on rap music.) But for my money, there's not a better name for a banker than Raymond Peepgass. And the tycoon's faux-plantation is named Turpmtine. Turp-em-tine. <br /><br />At first the characters' interactions are hopelessly stereotypical. The tycoon is egotistical and tone-deaf, causing the self-loathing banker to plot his downfall. The tycoon's ex wife, though living in glorious southern style, feels lonely and despondent in her middle years. Then, through a combination of implausible events, the lives of the worker and the tycoon collide and in this collision Wolfe reveals his moral message, which is (appropriately) couched in classical philosophy.<br /><br />The themes Wolfe chooses to address - race, power, greed, redemption, and the state of American society - are baldly exposed in the first few chapters of "A Man in Full." Wolfe loves front-line reportage, and his unsubtle style is one of his strengths. There are many spot-on observations in this book, and were it not for its similarity to "Bonfire of the Vanities," "A Man in Full" might have been more widely admired for its perceptiveness.<br /><br />But back to the size of the book. <br /><br />Set against a row of contemporary fiction, this hardcover makes other books look off-puttingly puny. It is big enough to give most 20th century fiction an inferiority complex. Yet it's not a book people keep around to brag about. It is an ambitious book and very engaging to read, but it's not a literary ground-breaker. It can't claim the creative cachet of a Pynchon or a Wallace. It contains no shocking conspiracy theories for readers to discuss at dinner parties; it doesn't "subvert the literary paradigm" or do anything radical with the language. It is, however, a very insightful and entertaining read, very much worth the 750 pages it fills. For its un-cynical examination of well-worn topics, it is a significant and relevant book worthy of anyone's shelves.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5522607888759715595">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2852813595014435109">I can't believe I bought this 740 page pop seller. It is so unlike what I usually read. I'm glad I broke out of the box and read it. It is well written, fascinating, thought provoking, delightful, fairly light reading, even at 700 pages. I read it in less than a week, as I just couldn't put it down. This is the first Tom Wolfe book I've read. <br /><br />The story evolves around 4 main characters, and one of them ends up in jail. I decided to skip many of the jail sections, as I cringed at the violence that</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2852813595014435109">I can't believe I bought this 740 page pop seller. It is so unlike what I usually read. I'm glad I broke out of the box and read it. It is well written, fascinating, thought provoking, delightful, fairly light reading, even at 700 pages. I read it in less than a week, as I just couldn't put it down. This is the first Tom Wolfe book I've read. <br /><br />The story evolves around 4 main characters, and one of them ends up in jail. I decided to skip many of the jail sections, as I cringed at the violence that was there. Wrong decision. It turns out that the jail pages are the glue that holds the whole book together. I've had to go back now, and read those pages, and although I hate reading about violence, it all makes sense. <br /><br />The main character is a 63 year old former football player, and his aging, and the aging of his first wife, the age of his second wife, are strongly presented in the book. It is funny, pathetic, and a part of life that I can completely empathize with. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_91232351">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91232351" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3338575-david-devalera" class="left imgcol" title="David DeValera"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1267033761p2/3338575.jpg" alt="David DeValera" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/91232351?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 26, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3338575-david-devalera" class="user" title="David DeValera">David DeValera</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer91232351">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4047160556688911538">Charlie Croker is one pig-headed cracker from the old-South. Charlie raises horses, fearlessly handles snakes, shoots quail, runs his own fleet of jets, is married to a younger, beautiful woman, and is in general a good ole boy -- even owns an honest ta gawd plantation where all the helpin' folk are black.<br /><br />Mr. Croker is also a man in prime need of a humbling experience. Charlie is a real estate developer and his most serious problems result from a wide-body ego coupled with backward planning: des</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4047160556688911538">Charlie Croker is one pig-headed cracker from the old-South. Charlie raises horses, fearlessly handles snakes, shoots quail, runs his own fleet of jets, is married to a younger, beautiful woman, and is in general a good ole boy -- even owns an honest ta gawd plantation where all the helpin' folk are black.<br /><br />Mr. Croker is also a man in prime need of a humbling experience. Charlie is a real estate developer and his most serious problems result from a wide-body ego coupled with backward planning: desire it, act on it--followed by--plan for it, pay for it. <br /><br />Charlie becomes overextended on a real estate deal for a development that is largely a monument to himself, even named it "The Croker Concourse." This leads to a "workout session" at Planners Bank, where Charlie is given a most unpleasant reception. Wolfe describes the scene in vintage style, casting a rheumy eye on corporate America and its ugly military efficiency and total bottom line orientation. It is at this point where you will realize that you have come to like Charlie Croker, that you are pulling for this humus head from south of the gnat line, that Croker, raw and crude as he is, contains a genuine spirit and optimism that has been squelched in the rest of us.<br /><br />Politics and money drive the entire story. Wolfe shows how saturated Americans are with these two Noble Truths. (Even Conrad-the-Stoic's actions, the character with the spiritual soul of this 787 page journey, were brought about by the frustrations of not being an economically viable member of society.) Wolfe's satire is as biting as a side ache, unfortunately, the truth running beneath the humor is a sobering one. This is the kind of book our grand kids will read and when they finish it, they will close the book and exclaim, "My god, were you people ever messed up!"<br /><br />I especially liked the chapters dealing with Atlanta's black mayor. He is like an inverted Oreo, posing publicly as white for the money constituents from the wealthy white neighborhoods, and posing privately as black for the less economically powerful, but more numerous black voters. The tribal art collection ebbs and flows through his office in accordance with the political tide.<br /><br />Wolfe brings the Mayor, Croker and Planners Bank together on an issue that threatens to explode the entire city in racial tension. Fareek Fanon, a black football star is accused of raping a white woman from one of Atlanta's most influential families. If Croker, (a former football great) speaks out on Fareek's behalf, maybe the mayor can help him with all his debts to Planners Bank? Maybe someone high up in Planners Bank will be owed a valuable gift in return for forgiving Crokers debt? And if the mayor quells the coming riots, maybe he can reclaim the straying voters and get reelected? <br /><br />Everyone is itching and planning for the scratch, but greed and political chess playing enter the equation, creating a centrifugal force that sucks some characters into the melee and spins others off into ruin.<br /><br />A Man in Full is ruthless and wicked writing from an author who portrays the face of American society with a magnifying glass held over the warts and moles. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4047160556688911538">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12000129209100884936">I read this about 8 years ago and started listening to it again a few weeks ago while I was doing some home improvment projects. I love tom Wolfe - I think he is one of the best story tellers ever and he can really spin a totally believable tale. He creates so much suspense and anxiety in very real-life kinds of situations. This is the story of 4 main characters as they come to find out what potential lies within each of them. Its great, but it does have a lot of harsh language and some sexual c</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12000129209100884936">I read this about 8 years ago and started listening to it again a few weeks ago while I was doing some home improvment projects. I love tom Wolfe - I think he is one of the best story tellers ever and he can really spin a totally believable tale. He creates so much suspense and anxiety in very real-life kinds of situations. This is the story of 4 main characters as they come to find out what potential lies within each of them. Its great, but it does have a lot of harsh language and some sexual content - mostly just with a horse breeding scene. He also gets to use the term solar-plexus in this book just like he does in all his others. Its my second favorite Wolfe book next to the Right Stuff.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1466841705">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1466841705" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1484029-mikey-b" class="left imgcol" title="Mikey B."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1424360925p2/1484029.jpg" alt="Mikey B." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1466841705?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 12, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1484029-mikey-b" class="user" title="Mikey B.">Mikey B.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1484029-mikey-b?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1466841705">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9413106607535919834">This is an uproarious and energized story of America. For over 700 pages Tom Wolfe sustains and enthralls you. The momentum never stops.<br /><br />There are myriad unforgettable confrontations and scenes – who can forget the bankers meeting, the jail settings (particularly the phone conversation), the breeding barn... The different classes of America meet and clash – super rich and middle class, black and white, clashes within black society (like who is blacker?), conservative and liberal, southerner and n</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9413106607535919834">This is an uproarious and energized story of America. For over 700 pages Tom Wolfe sustains and enthralls you. The momentum never stops.<br /><br />There are myriad unforgettable confrontations and scenes – who can forget the bankers meeting, the jail settings (particularly the phone conversation), the breeding barn... The different classes of America meet and clash – super rich and middle class, black and white, clashes within black society (like who is blacker?), conservative and liberal, southerner and northerner...<br /><br />Our main man is Charlie Croker. He is a flawed southern throwback whose real estate empire is collapsing and the banks are moving in for a relentless kill. He is over-loaded with a hubris that is under siege from all sides. There is a swirl of activity around him as city power factions attempt to manipulate him – to save the city of Atlanta from a potential racial conflagration.<br /><br />Mr. Wolfe is not afraid to play any volatile card - be it race, sex, politics or class standing (as in who you are, is what you possess – be it property, women, houses, an enterprise...).<br /><br />We get a wide kaleidoscopic canvas of American life at the end of the 20th Century. The settings vary from southern plantations, to ghettoes, to prisons and small suburban homes.<br /><br />As a caveat:<br />I am not sure which side Tom Wolfe is on – and I don’t think he favours any of his characters.<br />The book is very male-centric (something the author tried to correct with his next book <a title="I am Charlotte Simmons by Tom Wolfe" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/231.I_am_Charlotte_Simmons">I am Charlotte Simmons</a>)<br />Don’t look for long introspective meditation; that is not the style of Tom Wolfe.<br /><br />Page 643 (my book)<br /><br /><em>On almost every wall surface of the house were ... dolls and porcelain figurines. There were hundreds of them, possibly thousands. There were wooden knickknack shelves attached to the walls and filled with dolls, old ones, new ones, astronaut dolls, African dolls, Filipino dolls, Polynesian dolls. There were dolls resting on little knickknack shelves that went around each doorframe and every window, all chosen for the matching or harmonious colors of their clothes, dolls of old people as well as children, dolls of famous people such as Mark Twain, Genghis Khan, and Albert Einstein... The most beautifully colored ones – and some were extravagant examples of the porcelain maker’s art – were arranged on wooden knickknack shelves, like the dolls. The arrangements were precise. The gradations in size were pleasing. Nothing was out of place. Despite the vast number of objects, you were immediately struck by the unity and good taste of this exhibition of a lifetime of collecting.</em><br /><br />Page 543<br /><br /><em>[Ray] Peepgass opened his manila envelope and took out the elaborate full color brochure Croker Global had produced as the calling card for its leasing campaign. He let Harvey take a good look at the picture on the front. It was one of those architectural photographs that are so super-sharp in detail, they make you blink. The paper it was printed on was so thick, so rich, and creamy, it made you want to eat it.<br />Both of them, Harvey and Ray, once again comrades in arms after all those years, had leaned over so far to take a look at Croker’s cathedral of Mammon that was going for a song, their heads were almost touching. Each was thinking of the figures and building his own castles in the air.</em><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9413106607535919834">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_78521888">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78521888" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1309606-f-r" class="left imgcol" title="F.R."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1378152880p2/1309606.jpg" alt="F.R." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/78521888?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 24, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1309606-f-r" class="user" title="F.R.">F.R.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer78521888">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7961014098735831967">Firstly, one has to doff the cap to Tom Wolfe’s prose style. The writing throughout this long book remains at a consistently high level, and even chapters which I later considered superfluous were brilliantly written. Absolutely there were points where you could see his research poking through, segments where Wolfe proved he’d learnt something in such fine detail and wanted the reader to know that – but the fact that it was rendered so beautifully in English allowed me to accept these little lec</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7961014098735831967">Firstly, one has to doff the cap to Tom Wolfe’s prose style. The writing throughout this long book remains at a consistently high level, and even chapters which I later considered superfluous were brilliantly written. Absolutely there were points where you could see his research poking through, segments where Wolfe proved he’d learnt something in such fine detail and wanted the reader to know that – but the fact that it was rendered so beautifully in English allowed me to accept these little lectures. He is an excellent stylist.<br /><br />So I have no problems with the writing in ‘A Man in Full’, but I do have reservations about it as a work of fiction.<br /><br />Wolfe’s adherence to a Victorian novel is admirable, the old idea of looking at life at every strata and weaving these different strands all together. But I think in this instance he hasn’t brought these elements together that well, and I am suspicious as to how much empathy he has – even with his journalist’s hat on – with some of the areas he tackles. This is a book about a real estate tycoon named Charlie Croker who is about to go bankrupt; it’s also about an alleged rape by a black athlete of the daughter of a white businessman; it’s about black politics in Georgia; it’s about a man on the breadline falling foul of the law; it’s about dumped first wives; it’s about a banker whose life has crashed and latterly it’s about how the works of the Stoics can be made to apply in the late stages of the 20th century. I know that these varying strands do ostensibly come together at the end, but I wasn’t convinced by that convergence or by the conclusions reached from it. <br /><br />Furthermore, it’s clear Wolfe is more interested in writing about some areas than others. The rape case for instance – which at one point looks to be the driver of the book – is left on the shelf for chapter after chapter as the author concentrates on things he clearly finds more interesting. As such we have a lot of pages devoted to – in this book primarily set in Georgia – life behind bars in California. Now I thought those chapters were really well done, but were they truly necessary? Are they a comfortable fit in this book? Probably not, but as Wolfe had already done the research then he might as well work them in.<br /><br />Another problem I had was this a very male book. Yes, there are female characters, but they are only ever defined by the men in their lives. Okay, that’s true of a number of books, but Wolfe does bring one female character to the fore and still only uses her for nothing more than commenting on the men around her. That character is Charlie Croker’s first wife Martha, who is the most rounded female character in the novel, but still doesn’t feel rounded at all. And as for his second wife, the least said the better. (Has anyone read ‘I Am Charlotte Simmons’? The mind boggles.)<br /><br />All that being said, I enjoyed this book, even if I did have to stifle the odd yelp of criticism. I think though, that in a hundred years time, it will be the non-fiction rather than the novels that Wolfe is remembered for.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7961014098735831967">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13571228011122681382">This is a long book at 740 pages. The story builds rather well but the ending does a poor job of wrapping up the story lines. I was very disappointed. Its almost as if Wolfe lost interest in the book and just briefly summed everything up and not very inventively.<br /><br />I mainly read this book to get a feel for Wolfe writing style. Tom Wolfe is nothing if not good at character development. The level of detail he provides really allows you to see the scene in your mind and know the characters in the book</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13571228011122681382">This is a long book at 740 pages. The story builds rather well but the ending does a poor job of wrapping up the story lines. I was very disappointed. Its almost as if Wolfe lost interest in the book and just briefly summed everything up and not very inventively.<br /><br />I mainly read this book to get a feel for Wolfe writing style. Tom Wolfe is nothing if not good at character development. The level of detail he provides really allows you to see the scene in your mind and know the characters in the book. <br /><br />It seems that most of Wolfe's books surround a period of time such as Atlanta in the early 90's in this case. The story tries to portray the politics, mood, and traditions of that period. Almost as if the entire point of the book is to paint that picture in the readers mind rather than telling an effective story.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5335128-abel-mcbride?shelf=life-changers" class="actionLinkLite">life-changers</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer191807119">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9858674948457342659">Without a doubt the best book I've ever read in my literary career. Tom Wolfe is the sharpest mother fucker you'll ever read. He brings so many different elements into his writing. He captures the human spirit so excruciatingly well its a revelation to see it written in words. One his best aspects is although he is very literate and descriptive, his story telling is buttery smooth. I found myself blasting through this book, not only because the story, with all its sub plots and twists and turns,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9858674948457342659">Without a doubt the best book I've ever read in my literary career. Tom Wolfe is the sharpest mother fucker you'll ever read. He brings so many different elements into his writing. He captures the human spirit so excruciatingly well its a revelation to see it written in words. One his best aspects is although he is very literate and descriptive, his story telling is buttery smooth. I found myself blasting through this book, not only because the story, with all its sub plots and twists and turns, was superb, but because his writing is just easy to understand; like Tom Wolfe himself is retelling the account to your face. In the novel, a character finds himself relating eerily well with a book he randomly comes into contact with, and it helps him, drives him, makes him become a better version of himself. he sees getting the book as fate. I see it the exact same way. Never heard of tom wolfe before, got this book for Christmas, very random. but i gave it the benefit of the doubt and now i feel like, for some reason i cant even begin to ponder, i was supposed to get this book...that i was supposed to read it...that this is my life path and now I cant wait to indulge more into the wonderful ecstasy that is Tom Wolfe. That a one single mind conceived of this novel is nothing short of extraordinary. I can't recommend this book enough.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer244319050">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10143851789759633843">Tom Wolfe can write. The Right Stuff and Bonfire of the Vanities are two great examples. There are many bright spots in "A Man In Full", for instance, Conrad (the protagonist) ends up in Santa Rita jail, and must struggle surviving in that hostile environment. I thought that was quite well done until fate intervened and conveniently got him out of a bad situation. But all in all, the plot is so convoluted, and the prose is so filled with extraneous minutia, that I struggled to get through it. Th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10143851789759633843">Tom Wolfe can write. The Right Stuff and Bonfire of the Vanities are two great examples. There are many bright spots in "A Man In Full", for instance, Conrad (the protagonist) ends up in Santa Rita jail, and must struggle surviving in that hostile environment. I thought that was quite well done until fate intervened and conveniently got him out of a bad situation. But all in all, the plot is so convoluted, and the prose is so filled with extraneous minutia, that I struggled to get through it. This was my third try, having given up twice previously. Since it is one of my 2011 challenge books, I gave it another go, and finally finished it. The most disappointing thing is that this could have been so much better with some dedicated editing. Cut 200 pages and get to the point a lot quicker! This would probably be pretty good as a Readers Digest Condensed story. Can't recommend it otherwise. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_131083549">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/131083549" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4109080-david-prybil" class="left imgcol" title="David Prybil"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1281457111p2/4109080.jpg" alt="David Prybil" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/131083549?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 16, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4109080-david-prybil" class="user" title="David Prybil">David Prybil</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer131083549">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14574337343430577648">This book might not have captured the zeitgeist as fully as some of Wolfe's other books, but I think time will be very kind to it. In truth, I think it was ahead of its time, presaging the mortgage crisis and all the other excesses of this past decade. Some of the best, funniest, and most insightful writing I have had the pleasure of reading - ever. Just great stuff. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/1970656-toni-osborne" class="user" title="Toni Osborne">Toni Osborne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer45123143">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2021954527541306405">Charles (Charlie) Croker a middle-aged prominent Atlanta businessman finds his life turned up-side down when his ego brings him to a staggering debt load and to the brink of bankruptcy. Charlie is faced with laying off some workers at his food business to free up cash and buy some time. One victim is young Conrad Hensley who later becomes Charlie's therapist. His bankers smell blood, Raymond Peepgass has even secretly put together a syndicate to take over Crocker's office building at a cut rate.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2021954527541306405">Charles (Charlie) Croker a middle-aged prominent Atlanta businessman finds his life turned up-side down when his ego brings him to a staggering debt load and to the brink of bankruptcy. Charlie is faced with laying off some workers at his food business to free up cash and buy some time. One victim is young Conrad Hensley who later becomes Charlie's therapist. His bankers smell blood, Raymond Peepgass has even secretly put together a syndicate to take over Crocker's office building at a cut rate.<br /><br />Meanwhile Georgia star running back Fanon Fareek is accused of date raping the daughter of one of Charlie's society cronies, a pillar of the white establishment. Upscale black lawyer Roger White is asked to represent Fanon and doing so offers Charlie a deal that would get the bank off his back, it would mean speaking in favour of Fareek at a press conference.<br /><br />With the press conference looming Charlie must decide whether to go along with White's plan by praising Fareek and save his empire or risk losing everything and possibly causing a riot in Atlanta.<br /><br />The author narrates in this novel a myriad of details and social observations. Wolfe exposes pretension, hypocrisy, malice, greed and vices on top of the dynamism of contemporary life. This novel is a work of satire, utterly dark and brutal with moments of humour and complex emotions. I was immediately grabbed by the fabulous characters Wolfe introduced and the plot revolving around them, I could hardly put the book down.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2021954527541306405">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer4253852608572967761">This is one of my all time favourites. I read it when it came out and still remember parts of it vividly - Charlie Croker's almost invisible women, the rape, the meat factory, the jail, Plato and Socrates, the machinations of racial politics, and the kingly pride of real estate moguls who literally shape our cities.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText4253852608572967761">This is one of my all time favourites. I read it when it came out and still remember parts of it vividly - Charlie Croker's almost invisible women, the rape, the meat factory, the jail, Plato and Socrates, the machinations of racial politics, and the kingly pride of real estate moguls who literally shape our cities. I couldn't put this book down, and even though it slightly fell to pieces in the last fifty pages, it didn't matter when the previous 680 or so had kept me in thrall. It isn't as perfect a novel as The Bonfire of the Vanities, and you need to give up a few days of your life to read it - because once you start, you can't stop - but as with its predecessor, it beautifully captures the concerns, the perceptions, the foibles and the politics of the time.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_178243559">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/178243559" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz" class="left imgcol" title="David Lentz"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1447628103p2/3604115.jpg" alt="David Lentz" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/178243559?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 21, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz" class="user" title="David Lentz">David Lentz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer178243559">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1023233490432994337">The first 772 pages of this novel may rank among some of the best American mainstream fiction ever written. Wolfe certainly took his time in creating his opus maximus and his work ethic is worthy of great respect. I had the sense that Wolfe immersed himself in Atlanta society as the settings and characters seemed incredibly true to life. Wolfe's ear for American dialect showed great range and seemed unfailing in its ability to ring true. The leitmotifs to Epictetus added substance to the work. H</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1023233490432994337">The first 772 pages of this novel may rank among some of the best American mainstream fiction ever written. Wolfe certainly took his time in creating his opus maximus and his work ethic is worthy of great respect. I had the sense that Wolfe immersed himself in Atlanta society as the settings and characters seemed incredibly true to life. Wolfe's ear for American dialect showed great range and seemed unfailing in its ability to ring true. The leitmotifs to Epictetus added substance to the work. However, Wolfe went too far in the Epilogue and appears guilty of hubris in asking his devoted readers to succumb to the willing suspension of disbelief that he had laid at their feet. How can we accept the destinies Wolfe has shaped for the great Charlie Croker and Conrad? The essence of the message of Charlie Croker is wise and meaningful and truly American in the denouement. But he strains our credibility which was so wonderfully created in Wolfe's art up to the Epilogue. It would perhaps be the ultimate irony if the author in crafting his novel were guilty of the same hubris of his protagonist. Perhaps, if that's true, it only reinforces the verity of the tale and its wisdom. While I have great respect for the writing of Tom Wolfe, sometimes I just think he's too full of himself for words. Nevertheless, this is a great American tale which I shall always remember. Wolfe is a great American mainstream novelist. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1023233490432994337">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3447765024615992443">Wolfe's book reminded me, among other things, Julio Cortazar's Hopscotch, whose main scenario is Paris and in many cases it works like a guide of places, streets, and monuments of that European city. But A man in full is also a sociological, linguistical, political, journalistic novel. For me it was delicious have read this long tale of human behavior spiced with some many details about Atlanta and in general American way of life.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_109128997">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109128997" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3543747-kate" class="left imgcol" title="Kate"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1277754620p2/3543747.jpg" alt="Kate" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/109128997?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 28, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3543747-kate" class="user" title="Kate">Kate</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3543747-kate?shelf=southern-writing" class="actionLinkLite">southern-writing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer109128997">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3505480756290831280">I was introduced to Tom Wolfe through Dixon during my time at Furman. I read I am Charlotte Simmons and loved the way Wolfe was able to capture so many aspects of the college experience. I knew the characters he described, they just had different names and lived in my world instead.<br /><br />I learned very quickly that Wolfe has the keen ability to write about life in ways that make you think he experienced every subject he writes about--when he wrote The Right Stuff, he was John Glenn and described the o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3505480756290831280">I was introduced to Tom Wolfe through Dixon during my time at Furman. I read I am Charlotte Simmons and loved the way Wolfe was able to capture so many aspects of the college experience. I knew the characters he described, they just had different names and lived in my world instead.<br /><br />I learned very quickly that Wolfe has the keen ability to write about life in ways that make you think he experienced every subject he writes about--when he wrote The Right Stuff, he was John Glenn and described the orbit around the earth with stunning accuracy such that I felt butterflies when the shuttle was reentering the atmosphere with its heat shield partially damaged (I may be getting mixed up here on plot details so forgive me if I am incorrect--it may have been a different astronaut).<br /><br />I think that Wolfe's books have this quality because he is an incredible researcher who will spend years and years learning about the subjects in his books. When he was researching A Man in Full and knew he would have to write about men working in the freezer of a food distribution company, he experienced it himself so that he could write with perfect accuracy--and this is such a minor detail of the story!<br /><br />That being said, I have immense respect for Wolfe and know that when I read one of his books, I am certain to also learn facts about a variety of things that make up multiple human experiences that are quite real.<br /><br />A Man in Full tells the tale of Atlanta when it was just beginning to become the economic powerhouse that it is today through the character Charlie Croker. Croker embodies that stereotype that everybody knows or at least can relate to--the former football star and southern charismatic who makes it big in the real estate world and loses sight of who he is in the process.<br /><br />There are several other frame stories within the book with characters dealing with their own fates but who all somehow link back to Croker. I don't want to get too deep into the plot here, but I will say that the book is incredibly complex, but brilliantly complex such that every part that seems ancillary actually plays a significant role in the final outcome.<br /><br />It is exciting to see it all come together and I would especially recommend this book if you are from Atlanta because there are rumors floating about that several of the characters were based on actual figures.<br /><br />This is a book for those of you who love to see the greedy fall or have interests in race relations, politics and the mysterious ways of the South.<br /><br />There is a lot of build up to the plot in the beginning--mainly Wolfe setting his stage with at times extraneous details--but it is worth trudging through because the extra layers of complexity make the plot even more fascinating.<br /><br />"A Man in Full" was a joy to read and I would be willing to pass it on to anyone who would like to read it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3505480756290831280">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4963539283720685190">I remember that when I first picked up this book it was missing the first 30 pages. The reason is because someone had torn them out to use as toilet paper or to roll up joints or to keep score on during card games. This was in the federal detention center in Miami. I read it anyway. They actually had another copy of this book on the cart that looked like it was in new condition, but that copy was in Spanish. My spanish was intolerable at best, and I considered completing my spanish language lear</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4963539283720685190">I remember that when I first picked up this book it was missing the first 30 pages. The reason is because someone had torn them out to use as toilet paper or to roll up joints or to keep score on during card games. This was in the federal detention center in Miami. I read it anyway. They actually had another copy of this book on the cart that looked like it was in new condition, but that copy was in Spanish. My spanish was intolerable at best, and I considered completing my spanish language learning quest just to read the version that was untouched. That was impractical considering my situation, so I read the English version with the first 30 pages missing. I'm happy with my decision because this is an excellent read. One of the main characters even ends up in prison - something I could relate to. I liked the writing style and the different stoylines and how they tied together. I can see why Tom Wolfe is considered such a great author. A couple years later (still imprisoned) I actually convinced some other inmates to read this one and they did and they also enjoyed it. I also read a copy later on that had ALL the pages in it. Nice work Mr. Wolfe. </span>

        <a href="/review/show/1192863958?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 06, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7882762-ben" class="user" title="Ben">Ben</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1918615.A_Man_in_Full" title="Hardcover 9780224030366" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7882762-ben?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1192863958">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16726699711492468030">Tom Wolfe may not write great female characters, but he is a damned master at writing about the rise and fall of overly self important men in all strata of society. Corporate CEOs, politicians, middle management bankers, athletes, and working class schmoes - he takes us inside their heads where they share largely the same fears, worries, and insecurities. Can I make ends meet on the money I have? How can I get more? Does society respect me? Does my wife/girlfriend respect me, and if not, can I t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16726699711492468030">Tom Wolfe may not write great female characters, but he is a damned master at writing about the rise and fall of overly self important men in all strata of society. Corporate CEOs, politicians, middle management bankers, athletes, and working class schmoes - he takes us inside their heads where they share largely the same fears, worries, and insecurities. Can I make ends meet on the money I have? How can I get more? Does society respect me? Does my wife/girlfriend respect me, and if not, can I trade up? How can I take the next step ahead? Why does the world appear to be passing me by? What makes a man (in full)? Tom writes with a reporter's slavish detail to the facts of the plot and the story behind the facts. <br /><br />And, if you need another selling point, Chapter 3 is a true masterpiece where a pompous business exec defaulting on millions in loans shows up to a bankruptcy proceeding and gets taken down by a mid-level bank officer, piece by piece, and reduced to a sweating, cowering milquetoast. You read that chapter and think, if only this had happened to Enron and Goldman Sachs.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16726699711492468030">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer687191548">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1891978417876876582">I'll go ahead and admit it: he might not be a "great" writer, but I like Wolfe all the same. He has a smooth, flowing style and, though he tends to overindulge--I doubt this book needed to be 742 pages long to achieve the same effect--he fashions a compelling narrative. A little complexity and unpredictability couldn't hurt his characters, but nonetheless Wolfe makes you come away feeling somehow emotionally connected to even the most contemptible plutocrat. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1203238335" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8663519-richard-knight" class="left imgcol" title="Richard Knight"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442892701p2/8663519.jpg" alt="Richard Knight" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1203238335?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 16, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8663519-richard-knight" class="user" title="Richard Knight">Richard Knight</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1203238335">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13047885951123216045">Easily in the top five greatest books I've ever read, Tom Wolfe's, A Man in Full, is a modern day classic that has just as much to say about humanity as Uncle Tom's Cabin or The Jungle. I'm dead serious. Tom Wolfe did the impossible with this book by making every last character both likeable, as well as repugnant in their own little way. In other words, he made them into real human beings. This is why something as, unfortunately, commonplace as a rape case is turned into a sprawling (nearly 700</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13047885951123216045">Easily in the top five greatest books I've ever read, Tom Wolfe's, A Man in Full, is a modern day classic that has just as much to say about humanity as Uncle Tom's Cabin or The Jungle. I'm dead serious. Tom Wolfe did the impossible with this book by making every last character both likeable, as well as repugnant in their own little way. In other words, he made them into real human beings. This is why something as, unfortunately, commonplace as a rape case is turned into a sprawling (nearly 700 pages!) epic that feels justified for being so long. Every single page is essential to the last.<br /><br />I have to be honest, though. There was one chapter in particular that nearly threw me out of the book completely. While reading it, I thought the whole scenario was a dream sequence since it seemed so out of place. But by the end of the book, even that part, which comes out of nowhere (But makes sense given the location it takes place in), seems justified and essential to the rest of the story. <br /><br />In the end, I don't think I have ever read a book that has captivated me so much as A Man in Full. Each chapter is expertly strung together, and it's so brilliant, that you can't help but step back and marvel. Tom Wolfe is a genius, and this is his crowning achievement. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13047885951123216045">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_440274747">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/440274747" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/258010-chuck" class="left imgcol" title="Chuck"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" alt="Chuck" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/440274747?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 23, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/258010-chuck" class="user" title="Chuck">Chuck</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer440274747">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3695941284035618708">In many respects, this 1998 novel has a lot going for it. Tom Wolfe creates a convincing panorama of elite life in moneyed Atlanta, complete with a self-indulgent protagonist -- real estate magnate Charlie Croker -- who has risen to great heights by the time we meet him, and whose fall is richly documented over hundreds of pages. Juxtaposed against a cast of rich and powerful characters are a narcissistic college football player and a motley collection of felons and street-folk, all of whom spea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3695941284035618708">In many respects, this 1998 novel has a lot going for it. Tom Wolfe creates a convincing panorama of elite life in moneyed Atlanta, complete with a self-indulgent protagonist -- real estate magnate Charlie Croker -- who has risen to great heights by the time we meet him, and whose fall is richly documented over hundreds of pages. Juxtaposed against a cast of rich and powerful characters are a narcissistic college football player and a motley collection of felons and street-folk, all of whom speak in idiosyncratic dialects for which Wolfe seems to possess a well-tuned ear.<br /><br />Some strands of Wolfe's tale come together fairly naturally, but other elements require very substantial suspensions of disbelief -- a demand on the reader that only grows more onerous as the novel progresses toward its highly unlikely conclusion. Here's a capsule overview of the most implausible strand (I doubt that it can count as a "spoiler" for a popular novel published a dozen years ago): Charlie Croker's empire of wealth includes a global foods conglomerate, for which an underling employee -- Conrad Hensley -- works eight hour shifts in the freezer unit of a California warehouse. Through a convoluted sub-plot, Hensley loses his job and ends up in prison, but due to a fortuitous earthquake he escapes, eventually making his way to Atlanta via a 20th century underground railroad. And then the mother of all surprises: Hensley not only secures employment in the Croker household; he converts Charlie to a pseudo religion based upon the teachings of the philosopher Epictetus. This ultimately provides great solace to Charlie who, after being deprived entirely of his wealth and much of his health, goes on the road as an evangelist for Zeus, reportedly earning a goodly sum in his new endeavor. With the Stoics, apparently, all things are possible.<br /><br />Wolfe possesses a talent for narrative description, and his colorful swipes at bankers are particularly relevant and enjoyable in today's context. Overall, <em>A Man in Full</em> is eminently readable and entertaining, but one gets the sense that Wolfe continued his story for so long that he just couldn't find an artistically satisfying way of ending it. Sometimes less really is more.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3695941284035618708">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer58091337">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9844591058858174474">In 2007 I discovered a weathered copy of this novel on a bookshelf in a Mexican hotel and read it straight through on the long trip home. This hotly anticipated follow-up to "Bonfire of the Vanities" hit bookstores with a thud when it was released. Here's why: 1) Atlanta ain't New York as Grand Canvass for an epic; 2) the book is 50% too long. <br /><br />But I loved the story of Charlie Croker, the "Man in Full" maverick real estate developer nonetheless. I often work with developers and Wolfe nailed a cer</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9844591058858174474">In 2007 I discovered a weathered copy of this novel on a bookshelf in a Mexican hotel and read it straight through on the long trip home. This hotly anticipated follow-up to "Bonfire of the Vanities" hit bookstores with a thud when it was released. Here's why: 1) Atlanta ain't New York as Grand Canvass for an epic; 2) the book is 50% too long. <br /><br />But I loved the story of Charlie Croker, the "Man in Full" maverick real estate developer nonetheless. I often work with developers and Wolfe nailed a certain type of character in all his excesses. <br /><br />And now with the demise of real estate (at least temporarily, but who knows?) I see people going through "workouts" and caving into "the bags." Plus this book is seriously funny ... the "boys with breasts" fitness-club chapter; the epic horse-insemination scene.<br /><br />Have I whet(ted?) your appetite yet? If only an editor had the gumption to edit Wolfe this would be a classic. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1320450488">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1320450488" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/12727051-spike-gomes" class="left imgcol" title="Spike Gomes"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1347342213p2/12727051.jpg" alt="Spike Gomes" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1320450488?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 29, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/12727051-spike-gomes" class="user" title="Spike Gomes">Spike Gomes</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1320450488">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14433649402692147888">As with any Tom Wolfe novel, this is a novel of characters. A millionaire business owner up to his neck in bad debt. A desperate working class guy who spirals into the pit of ruin and despair. A middle-aged black lawyer desperate to escape the taint of his white tastes and education. A milquetoast banker who finally decides to go after the big score. A scorned aging first wife, looking for relevance in her post-divorce world. All set in the Boardrooms, country estates, ghettos and prisons of tur</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14433649402692147888">As with any Tom Wolfe novel, this is a novel of characters. A millionaire business owner up to his neck in bad debt. A desperate working class guy who spirals into the pit of ruin and despair. A middle-aged black lawyer desperate to escape the taint of his white tastes and education. A milquetoast banker who finally decides to go after the big score. A scorned aging first wife, looking for relevance in her post-divorce world. All set in the Boardrooms, country estates, ghettos and prisons of turn of the century modern America.<br /><br />Honestly, no one can write a social novel like Wolfe can. Unfortunately, it seems like he is a master losing his edge to age and his own conceits. In some ways this is a greater novel than "Bonfire of the Vanities". The characters are more filled out, the plotting is more gripping, the descriptive details more pointed and varied, the ideas more fully formed and developed... yet we see the seeds of his decline, and the weaknesses of the two novels following. He doesn't *get* young people or the more modern developments of the internet and pop culture, and his bewilderment expresses itself as a rather inchoate hostility rather than the nuanced pointed satire we expect of him. The novel also hinges on a plot lynchpin involving Stoic philosophy which seems forced and fantastic compared to the studied realism of the character portraits. That said, both make great reading and are colorfully done, but a plausible cultural critique? Not in the least.<br /><br />One hopes that someone picks up his torch, someone who can provide an honest and true to life scathing social satire of everyone under the age of 40 as he did to the middle aged and past their prime characters in this novel. We certainly need someone to write the great social novel lambasting the "geniuses" of internet start-ups in Silicon Valley, the parasitical hysterics of Gawkeresque social media hipster gadflies, and the struggles of normal young people to find a job under crushing college debt or lack of pointless degree credentials. It likely won't be him in his fusty suits and rested laurels.<br /><br />That said, this is a beautiful swan song from the master.<br /><br />4 out of 5 stars. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14433649402692147888">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/17464957-georgia" class="user" title="Georgia">Georgia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>
