        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1137309841">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11250493621401892130">Entertaining, but not as good as Bonfire of the Vanities or the Right Stuff. The central storyline is broadly similar to Bonfire: a rich real-estate mogul gets himself into a situation that threatens his personal and professional standing in well-to-do Atlanta. Seemingly small decisions and fabrications start a Fawlty Towers-esque chain of events that build up to a confrontation.<br /><br /> Wolfe often uses caricature to highlight the anxieties and insecurities of his characters, but in this book, the plot</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11250493621401892130">Entertaining, but not as good as Bonfire of the Vanities or the Right Stuff. The central storyline is broadly similar to Bonfire: a rich real-estate mogul gets himself into a situation that threatens his personal and professional standing in well-to-do Atlanta. Seemingly small decisions and fabrications start a Fawlty Towers-esque chain of events that build up to a confrontation.<br /><br /> Wolfe often uses caricature to highlight the anxieties and insecurities of his characters, but in this book, the plot and situations were too absurd for me to really identify with any of them. The plot was much too reliant on coincidence. <br /><br />As an aside, homosexuality is shown in a pretty unsympathetic light throughout the book. Given that there are no (explicitly) gay characters and very few women, it was weird that gay sex, homosexual rape and dismissive chitchat about gay rights figured in the plot so many times. Perhaps this just shows that the 'Zeitgeist' will necessarily look a little uglier once its time has passed...<br /><br /><br /></span>

  <a href="/user/show/22929304-owen" class="left imgcol" title="Owen"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" alt="Owen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1102534526?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 10, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/22929304-owen" class="user" title="Owen">Owen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/86172.A_Man_in_Full" title="Paperback 9780553381337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1102534526">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4393728501545775755">An engaging and engrossing book but a bit overlong - could have done with some editing.<br /><br />Tom Wolfe is a funny and sharp writer and, as he does in the more famous Bonfire of the Vanities, he sets out several strands of a story then brings them together as the book unfolds. The themes are also familiar from Bonfire - race; inequalities of wealth; sex and sexism; and politics, and its relationship with money.<br /><br />The novel is set mainly in Atlanta and much fun is had with sardonic and ironic descriptions</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4393728501545775755">An engaging and engrossing book but a bit overlong - could have done with some editing.<br /><br />Tom Wolfe is a funny and sharp writer and, as he does in the more famous Bonfire of the Vanities, he sets out several strands of a story then brings them together as the book unfolds. The themes are also familiar from Bonfire - race; inequalities of wealth; sex and sexism; and politics, and its relationship with money.<br /><br />The novel is set mainly in Atlanta and much fun is had with sardonic and ironic descriptions of Southern stereotypes.<br /><br />But this is a very entertaining book, simply because the style carries you along and he brilliantly puts you in the mind of the character while at the same time showing you how wrong-headed they are. It is a kind of comedy of manners.<br /><br />My criticism is that some of the big set piece episodes and dialogues go on a bit too long. There is obviously a fine balance to be struck between not scrimping on the description and detail that are so necessary to create the dramatic tension, and just dragging things out a bit too much.<br /><br />But this is a good book for a holiday or a time when you can take it in big bite-sized chunks.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4393728501545775755">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1077619237">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12437772809960738444">Tom Wolfe dissects sub-cultures with such skill, he may be one of America’s best anthropologists. Better than almost any other author, he can convey the mores, manners, motivations, and behaviors of cliques and groups. Wolfe seems to absorb the beliefs and practices of people who by birth/geography or self-selection live within a cluster of similarly minded people. This is a rare gift … and one that is over-used in A Man in Full. I’m biased toward the art of storytelling, and A Man in Full seems</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12437772809960738444">Tom Wolfe dissects sub-cultures with such skill, he may be one of America’s best anthropologists. Better than almost any other author, he can convey the mores, manners, motivations, and behaviors of cliques and groups. Wolfe seems to absorb the beliefs and practices of people who by birth/geography or self-selection live within a cluster of similarly minded people. This is a rare gift … and one that is over-used in A Man in Full. I’m biased toward the art of storytelling, and A Man in Full seems like a tour de force portrayal of different sub-cultures in search of a story. Not that there isn’t a story—it’s just that it was not a satisfying story in the sense that it felt complete with adequate resolution of the conflicts. Much of the problem is the end, which is so abrupt it made this reader feel discarded. Wolfe wraps up all of the countless loose ends with two characters chatting about all of the happenings after the climactic scene. The dialogue is all tell … and far too contrived. Tell presented as dialogue is still tell. I was waiting for something really grand to finish off a literary meal but instead got a toll house cookie.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12437772809960738444">...more</a>

          </span>

In this imp&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText15565692798712881507\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;&amp;quot;True North&amp;quot; shows how anyone who follows their internal compass can become an authentic leader. This leadership tour de force is based on research and first-person interviews with 125 of today\'s top leaders--with some surprising results. In this important book, acclaimed former Medtronic CEO Bill George and coauthor Peter Sims share the wisdom of these outstanding leaders and describe how you can develop as an authentic leader. &amp;quot;True North&amp;quot; presents a concrete and comprehensive program for leadership success and shows how to create your own Personal Leadership Development Plan centered on five key areas: Knowing your authentic self Defining your values and leadership principles Understanding your motivations Building your support team Staying grounded by integrating all aspects of your life &amp;quot;True North&amp;quot; offers an opportunity for anyone to transform their leadership path and become the authentic leader they were born to be.\n\nPersonal, original, and illuminating stories from Warren Bennis, Sir Adrian Cadbury, George Shultz (former U.S. secretary of state), Charles Schwab, John Whitehead (Cochairman, Goldman Sachs), Anne Mulcahy (CEO, Xerox), Howard Schultz (CEO, Starbucks), Dan Vasella (CEO, Novartis), John Brennan (Chairman, Vanguard), Carol Tome (CFO, Home Depot), Donna Dubinsky (CEO/cofounder, Palm), Alan Horn (President, Warner Brothers), Ann Moore (CEO, Time, Inc.) and many others illustrate the transitions that shape the type of leaders who will thrive in the 21st century.\n\nBill George (Cambridge, MA) has spent over 30 years in executive leadership positions at Litton, Honeywell, and Medtronic. As CEO of Medtronic, he built the company into the world\'s leading medical technology company as its market capitalization increased from $1.1 billion to $60 billion. Since 2004, he has been a professor at the Harvard Business School. His 2004 book Authentic Leadership (0-7879-7528-1) was a &amp;quot;BusinessWeek&amp;quot; bestseller. Peter Sims (San Francisco, CA) established &amp;quot;Leadership Perspectives,&amp;quot; a course on leadership development at the Stanford Graduate School of Business and cofounded the London office of Summit Partners, a leading investment firm.\n\nTheir Web site is www.truenorthleaders.com.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"15565692798712881507\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent($(this));; <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10981.Novels_1936_1940"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/books/1347365307m/10981.jpg" alt="Novels, 1936-1940" />

<script>
//&lt;![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_10981'), "\n\n  &lt;h2&gt;&lt;a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10981.Novels_1936_1940?from_choice=false&amp;amp;from_home_module=false\"&gt;Novels, 1936-1940&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\n\n      &lt;div&gt;\n        by &lt;a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/3535.William_Faulkner?a=7&amp;amp;origin=related_works\"&gt;William Faulkner&lt;\/a&gt;\n      &lt;\/div&gt;\n\n          &lt;div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\"&gt;\n            &lt;span class=\"minirating\"&gt;&lt;span class=\"stars staticStars\"&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;\/span&gt; 4.45 avg rating &amp;mdash; 195 ratings&lt;\/span&gt;            &amp;mdash; published 1990\n          &lt;\/div&gt;\n\n    &lt;div class=\"addBookTipDescription\"&gt;\n      \n&lt;span id=\"freeTextContainer2234874395434049812\"&gt;These four novels from the 1930s show Faulkner at the height of his powers and fully demonstrate the range of his genius. They explore the tragic and comic aspects of a South haunted by its past and uncertain of its future.In the intricate, spellbind&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText2234874395434049812\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;These four novels from the 1930s show Faulkner at the height of his powers and fully demonstrate the range of his genius. They explore the tragic and comic aspects of a South haunted by its past and uncertain of its future.In the intricate, spellbinding masterpiece Absalom, Absalom! (1936), Quentin Compson descends into a vortex of images, voices, passions, and doomed desires as he and his Harvard roommate re-create the story of Thomas Sutpen and the insane ambitions, romantic hopes, and distortions of honor and conscience that trap Sutpen and those around him, until their grief and pride and fate become the inescapable and unbearable legacy of a past that is not dead and not even past.In seven episodes, The Unvanquished (1938) recounts the ordeals and triumphs of the Sartoris family during and after the Civil War as seen through the maturing consiousness of young Bayard Sartoris. The indomitable Granny Millard, the honor-driven patriarch Colonel Sartoris, the quick-witted and inventive Ringo, the ferociously heroic Drusilla, and the scheming, mendacious Ab Snopes embody the inheritance that Bayard must reconcile a with new, but diminished, South.If I Forget Thee, Jerusalem (published in 1939 as The Wild Palms) tells of desperate lovers fleeing convention and of a convict escaping the chaos of passion. In &amp;quot;The Wild Palms,&amp;quot; an emotional and geographic odyssey ends in a Mississippi coastal town. In counterpoint, &amp;quot;Old Man&amp;quot; recounts the adventures of an inarticulate &amp;quot;tall convict&amp;quot; swept to freedom by a raging Mississippi flood, but who then fights to return to his simple prison life.In The Hamlet (1940), the first book of the great Snopes family trilogy, the outrageous scheming energy of Flem Snopes and his relatives is vividly and hilariously juxtaposed with the fragil communal customs of Frenchman\'s Bend. Here are Ike Snopes, in love with a cow, the sexual adventures of Eula Varner Snopes, and the wild saturnalia of the spotted horses auction, a comic masterpiece. The Library of America edition of Faulkner\'s work publishes for the first time new, corrected texts of The Unvanquished,If I Forget Thee, Jerusalem, and The Hamlet. The corrected text of Absalom,Absalom! was published by Random House in 1986. Manuscripts, typescripts, galleys, and published editions have been collated to produce versions that are faithful to Faulkner\'s intentions and free of the changes introduced by subsequent     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3083854.Tom_Wolfe">About Tom Wolfe</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/3083854.Tom_Wolfe"><img alt="3083854" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1368273666p2/3083854.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer2245994210646546524">Wolfe was educated at Washington and Lee Universities and also at Yale, where he received a PhD in American studies. <br /><br />Tom Wolfe spent his early days as a <em>Washington Post</em> beat reporter, where his free-association, onomatopoetic style would later become the trademark of New Journalism. In books such as <em>The Electric Koolaid Acid Test, The Right Stuff</em>, and <em>The Bonfire of the Vanities</em>, Wolfe delves into</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2245994210646546524">Wolfe was educated at Washington and Lee Universities and also at Yale, where he received a PhD in American studies. <br /><br />Tom Wolfe spent his early days as a <em>Washington Post</em> beat reporter, where his free-association, onomatopoetic style would later become the trademark of New Journalism. In books such as <em>The Electric Koolaid Acid Test, The Right Stuff</em>, and <em>The Bonfire of the Vanities</em>, Wolfe delves into the inner workings of the mind, writing about the unconscious decisions people make in their lives. His attention to eccentricities of human behavior and language and to questions of social status are considered unparalleled in the American literary canon.<br /><br /><br />He is one of the founders of the <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Journalism">New Journalism</a> movement of the 1960s and 1970s.<br /><br />Tom Wolfe is also famous for coining and defining the term <em>
  <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiction-absolute">fiction-absolute</a>
</em>.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://us.macmillan.com/author/tomwolfe" target="_blank">http://us.macmillan.com/author/tomwolfe</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2245994210646546524">...more</a>

    </div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1997693-a-man-in-full">Quotes from A Man in Full</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“One of the few freedoms that we have as human beings that cannot be taken away from us is the freedom to assent to what is true and to deny what is false. Nothing you can give me is worth surrendering that freedom for. At this moment I'm a man with complete tranquillity...I've been a real estate developer for most of my life, and I can tell you that a developer lives with the opposite of tranquillity, which is perturbation. You're perturbed about something all the time. You build your first development, and right away you want to build a bigger one, and you want a bigger house to live in, and if it ain't in Buckhead, you might as well cut your wrists. Soon's you got that, you want a plantation, tens of thousands of acres devoted solely to shooting quail, because you know of four or five developers who've already got that. And soon's you get that, you want a place on Sea Island and a Hatteras cruiser and a spread northwest of Buckhead, near the Chattahoochee, where you can ride a horse during the week, when you're not down at the plantation, plus a ranch in Wyoming, Colorado, or Montana, because truly successful men in Atlanta and New York all got their ranches, and of course now you need a private plane, a big one, too, a jet, a Gulfstream Five, because who's got the patience and the time and the humility to fly commercially, even to the plantation, much less out to a ranch? What is it you're looking for in this endless quest? Tranquillity. You think if only you can acquire enough worldly goods, enough recognition, enough eminence, you will be free, there'll be nothing more to worry about, and instead you become a bigger and bigger slave to how you think others are judging you.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/632977-one-of-the-few-freedoms-that-we-have-as-human" class="actionLinkLite">13 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“He Who Would Live Forever had done an instantaneous back-of-the-envelope calculation and decided that the vicinity of the Chevrolet Suburban was a better strategic alternative than anyplace anywhere near that whitish sandy road above which a gigantic terror-chattering rattlesnake now thrashed in the grip of his boss gone berserk.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/558724-he-who-would-live-forever-had-done-an-instantaneous-back-of-the-envelope" class="actionLinkLite">5 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
